The revolution was a direct result of the Islamic revival, led by an [Islamist](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamist "Islamist"), Ayatollah [Ruhollah Khomeini](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ruhollah_Khomeini "Ruhollah Khomeini"), who was very much in favor of Islamic unity, and "the leadership position that went with it".<sup id="cite_ref-121"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup> At first the revolution inspired and energized [Islamist](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamist "Islamist") Muslims (both Shia and Sunni) everywhere, but it was a revolution in a predominantly Shi'i Muslim country, led by Shi'i Muslims, and serious rifts with Sunni Muslims soon developed.

The revolution changed the Shia–Sunni power equation in Muslim countries "from Lebanon to India". It aroused the traditionally subservient Shia, to the alarm of traditionally dominant and very non-revolutionary Sunni.<sup id="cite_ref-nasr-2006-148_112-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-nasr-2006-148-112">[112]</a></sup> "Where Iranian revolutionaries saw Islamic revolutionary stirrings, Sunnis saw mostly Shia mischief and a threat to Sunni predominance."<sup id="cite_ref-122"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup>

Notwithstanding the desire of Iran's leader, Khomeini, for Shia–Sunni unity, as an Islamist revolutionary flush with success that had surprised Iranians as well as the rest of the world, Khomeini now sought the overthrow of unworthy governments in Muslim-majority countries (which were all Sunni regimes except for [Ibadi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ibadi_Islam "Ibadi Islam")\-led [Oman](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oman "Oman"), Khomeini's government being the only Shi'i-led country at the time). Pro-American monarchies in particular were high on that list, and at the very top was the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia, not only a Wahhabi state with a long tradition of anti-Shi'ism, but an "American lackey" and "unpopular and corrupt dictatorship" (in his view, especially after seeing the 1979 [Grand Mosque seizure](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grand_Mosque_seizure "Grand Mosque seizure")), ripe for revolution, like "a ripe apple ready to fall into" their hands.<sup id="cite_ref-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.150_123-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.150-123">[122]</a></sup> But Saudi Arabia was also spending billions of dollars every year [funding](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_propagation_of_Salafism_and_Wahhabism "International propagation of Salafism and Wahhabism") Islamic schools, scholarships, and fellowships, mosques around the Sunni world. "Thousands of aspiring preachers, Islamic scholars, and activists from Nigeria to Indonesia went to Saudi Arabia to study, and many more joined Saudi-funded think tanks and research institutions." They "then spread throughout the Muslim world to teach" what they had learned and "work at Saudi-funded universities, schools, mosques, and research institutions."<sup id="cite_ref-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.155_124-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.155-124">[123]</a></sup> Khomeini's attack was opposed not only by the Saudi royal family but by its many (Sunni) fundamentalist allies and benefactors throughout the Arab world. For them the House of Saud was very popular, a leader of Islamic revival.<sup id="cite_ref-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.143-4_125-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.143-4-125">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.155_124-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.155-124">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-126">[note 2]</a></sup> Saudi propaganda efforts proceeded to go after both Khomeini's Shia identity,<sup id="cite_ref-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.150_123-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.150-123">[122]</a></sup> and to "drive all possible wedges between Sunnism and Shiism".<sup id="cite_ref-Nasr,_''Shia_Revival'',_2006,_p.157_127-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Nasr,_''Shia_Revival'',_2006,_p.157-127">[125]</a></sup>

Another indirect effect (noted by political scientist [Gilles Kepel](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gilles_Kepel "Gilles Kepel")), was that however religious the Saudi regime was already, in the immediate wake of Iran's Revolution it was motivated to further shore up its "religious legitimacy" with more strictness in religion (and with jihad in Afghanistan) to compete with the grassroots enthusiasm for Iran's Islamism.<sup id="cite_ref-GKJTPI2002:137_128-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-GKJTPI2002:137-128">[126]</a></sup> But this also meant moving in a more anti-Shia religious direction because (as mentioned above) Saudi's own native Sunni school of Islam ([Wahhabism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wahhabism "Wahhabism"), like that of [Ibn Taymiyyah](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ibn_Taymiyyah#Shi'a_Islam "Ibn Taymiyyah")), did not consider Shiism part of the diversity of Islam, but a heresy to be fought. This new strictness was spread among the thousands of students in Saudi funded schools and more importantly among the international Islamist volunteers who came to training camps in Peshawar Pakistan in the 1980s to learn to fight jihad against Marxist secularists in Afghanistan and went home to fight jihad in the 1990s. Both groups (especially in Iraq and Pakistan) saw Shia as the enemy.<sup id="cite_ref-GKJTPI2002:142-3_129-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-GKJTPI2002:142-3-129">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gonzalez-2009-78_130-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Gonzalez-2009-78-130">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Murphy-2013-97_131-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Murphy-2013-97-131">[129]</a></sup>

Other Sunni Muslim states—Indonesia, Egypt—also "quickly moved" to bolster their Islamic credentials<sup id="cite_ref-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.149_132-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.149-132">[130]</a></sup> and inoculate themselves from the fate of the shah, aware of the plans the Iranian Islamist revolutionaries had for their downfall.<sup id="cite_ref-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.150_123-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.150-123">[122]</a></sup> A number of incidents convinced several Muslim heads of state (again, all Sunni) of Khomeini's contempt for them and the need to "contain" him:<sup id="cite_ref-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.141_133-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.141-133">[131]</a></sup> A delegation of Muslim heads of state that came to Tehran to mediate an end to the Iran-Iraq war was kept waiting for two hours before Khomeini appeared to make a ten minute untranslated statement seated while his visitors stood—and then leaving;<sup id="cite_ref-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.141_133-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.141-133">[131]</a></sup> a street in Tehran was named after the killer of the President of Egypt, [Anwar Sadat](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anwar_Sadat "Anwar Sadat");<sup id="cite_ref-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.143_134-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.143-134">[132]</a></sup> a threat by Khomeini to do to Pakistani President [Zia ul-Haq](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zia_ul-Haq "Zia ul-Haq")—a pious conservative Muslim seeking to Islamize Pakistan -- "what he had done to the Shah" if Zia mistreated the Shia in Pakistan,<sup id="cite_ref-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.138_101-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.138-101">[101]</a></sup> and on another occasion mockery of Zia's warning not to provoke a superpower by saying he, (Khomeini), had his own superpower – his being God while Zia's was the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Nasr,_Vali_2006_pp.161-2_102-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Nasr,_Vali_2006_pp.161-2-102">[102]</a></sup>

Following the Iranian Revolution, "avowedly Shia political movements", often getting funding from the IRI, and "pushing specifically Shia political agendas",<sup id="cite_ref-135"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-135">[133]</a></sup> emerged in to 2015, Shia groups in Lebanon, Iraq, Syria, Yemen, supported by Iran. By 2015 they had won "important political victories" which have boosted Iran's regional influence.<sup id="cite_ref-CFR-SSD_105-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-CFR-SSD-105">[105]</a></sup> In Lebanon, [Hezbollah](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hezbollah "Hezbollah"), the Lebanese Shia militia and political movement is the "strongest political actor" in the country. Since the 2003 [invasion of Iraq](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Invasion_of_Iraq "Invasion of Iraq") removed [Saddam Hussein](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saddam_Hussein "Saddam Hussein") from power and instituted elected government, the Shia majority has dominated the parliament and its prime ministers have been Shia.<sup id="cite_ref-CFR-SSD_105-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-CFR-SSD-105">[105]</a></sup> In Syria, a Shia minority—the heterodox Alawi sect that makes up only about 13 percent of the population—dominate the upper reaches of the government, military and security services in Syria, and are the "backbone" of the forces fighting to protect the [Bashar al-Assad](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bashar_al-Assad "Bashar al-Assad") regime in Syria's civil war.<sup id="cite_ref-CFR-SSD_105-5"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-CFR-SSD-105">[105]</a></sup> In Yemen, [Houthi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Houthis "Houthis") rebels have expanded their territory south of Saudi Arabia, and become the country's "[dominant power](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Houthi_insurgency_in_Yemen "Houthi insurgency in Yemen")".<sup id="cite_ref-CFR-SSD_105-6"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-CFR-SSD-105">[105]</a></sup>

### US invasion of Iraq [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations&action=edit&section=36 "Edit section: US invasion of Iraq")

Among those blaming the US invasion of Iraq for the growth in sectarianism are Fawaz Gerges, who writes in his book _ISIS: A History_,

> By destroying state institutions and establishing a sectarian-based political system, the 2003 US-led invasion polarized the country along Sunni-Shia lines and set the stage for a fierce, prolonged struggle driven by identity politics. Anger against the United States was also fueled by the humiliating disbandment of the Iraqi army and the de-Baathification law, which was first introduced as a provision and then turned into a permanent article of the constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-NYRB-Ruthven_113-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-NYRB-Ruthven-113">[113]</a></sup>

Malise Ruthven writes that the post invasion [de-Ba'athification](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/De-Ba%27athification "De-Ba'athification") by the US occupiers deprived Iraq of "the officer class and administrative cadres that had ruled under Saddam Hussein, leaving the field to sectarian-based militias".<sup id="cite_ref-NYRB-Ruthven_113-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-NYRB-Ruthven-113">[113]</a></sup> Many of officers joined the anti-Shia [takfiri](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Takfiri "Takfiri") [ISIL](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISIL "ISIL") group.

The US-led invasion also “tilted the regional balance of power decisively" in favor of Shia Iran, alarming Sunni and leading to talk of a “Shia Crescent”.<sup id="cite_ref-NYRB-Ruthven_113-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-NYRB-Ruthven-113">[113]</a></sup>

### Counter-revolutionary tactic [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations&action=edit&section=37 "Edit section: Counter-revolutionary tactic")

[Marc Lynch](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marc_Lynch "Marc Lynch") in his book _The New Arab Wars: Uprisings and Anarchy in the Middle East_, argues that as old regimes or political forces sought to control "the revolutionary upsurge" of the [Arab Spring](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Spring "Arab Spring"), [sectarianism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sectarianism "Sectarianism") became “a key weapon” to undermine unity among the anti-regime masses. Christians were pitted "against Muslims in Egypt, Jordanians against Palestinians in Jordan, and, above all, Sunnis against Shi’ites wherever possible.”<sup id="cite_ref-NYRB-Ruthven_113-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-NYRB-Ruthven-113">[113]</a></sup>

## Relations by country and region [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations&action=edit&section=38 "Edit section: Relations by country and region")

### Iraq [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations&action=edit&section=39 "Edit section: Iraq")

Shia–Sunni discord in Iraq starts with disagreement over the relative population of the two groups. The governing regimes of Iraq were composed mainly of Sunnis for nearly a century until the 2003 Iraq War, but according to most sources, the majority of the population is Shia. The CIA's _[World Factbook](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_World_Factbook "The World Factbook")_, estimates Shia [Arab Muslims](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Muslims "Arab Muslims") as making up 60% of Iraqis, and Sunni [muslims](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muslims "Muslims") 37%.<sup id="cite_ref-136"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-136">[134]</a></sup> However, Sunni are split ethnically among [Arabs](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_people "Arab people"), [Kurds](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kurdish_people "Kurdish people") and [Turkmen](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iraqi_Turkmens "Iraqi Turkmens"). Many Sunnis hotly dispute their minority status, (including ex-Iraqi Ambassador Faruq Ziada),<sup id="cite_ref-Ziada-2006_137-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Ziada-2006-137">[135]</a></sup> and many believe Shia majority is "a myth spread by America".<sup id="cite_ref-138"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup> One Sunni belief shared by Jordan's [King Abdullah](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abdullah_II_of_Jordan "Abdullah II of Jordan") as well as his then Defense Minister Shaalan is that Shia numbers in Iraq were inflated by Iranian Shia crossing the border.<sup id="cite_ref-139"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-139">[137]</a></sup> Shia scholar Vali Nasr believes the election turnout in summer and December 2005 confirmed a strong Shia majority in Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-140"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-140">[138]</a></sup>

The British, having put down a Shia rebellion against their rule in the 1920s, "confirmed their reliance on a corps of Sunni ex-officers of the collapsed Ottoman empire". The British [colonial](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colonialism "Colonialism") rule ended after the Sunni and Shia united against it.<sup id="cite_ref-141"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-141">[139]</a></sup>

The Shia suffered indirect and direct [persecution](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Persecution_of_Shia_Muslims "Persecution of Shia Muslims") under post-colonial Iraqi governments since 1932, erupting into [full-scale rebellions](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iraqi_Shia_revolts_1935%E2%80%931936 "Iraqi Shia revolts 1935–1936") in 1935 and 1936. Shia were also persecuted during the [Ba'ath Party](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ba%27ath_Party "Ba'ath Party") rule, especially under [Saddam Hussein](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saddam_Hussein "Saddam Hussein"). It is said that every Shia clerical family of note in Iraq had tales of torture and murder to recount.<sup id="cite_ref-142"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-142">[140]</a></sup> In 1969 the son of Iraq's highest Shia Ayatollah [Muhsin al-Hakim](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muhsin_al-Hakim "Muhsin al-Hakim") was arrested and allegedly tortured. From 1979 to 1983 Saddam's regime executed 48 major Shia clerics in Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-143"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-143">[141]</a></sup> They included Shia leader [Mohammad Baqir al-Sadr](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mohammad_Baqir_al-Sadr "Mohammad Baqir al-Sadr") and his sister. Tens of thousands of Iranians and Arabs of Iranian origin were expelled in 1979 and 1980 and a further 75,000 in 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-144"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-144">[142]</a></sup>

The Shia openly [revolted against Saddam](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/1991_uprisings_in_Iraq "1991 uprisings in Iraq") following the [Gulf War](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gulf_War "Gulf War") in 1991 and were encouraged by Saddam's defeat in Kuwait and by simultaneous Kurdish uprising in the north. However, Shia opposition to the government was brutally suppressed, resulting in some 50,000 to 100,000 casualties and successive repression by Saddam's forces.<sup id="cite_ref-145"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-145">[143]</a></sup>

#### Iraq War [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations&action=edit&section=40 "Edit section: Iraq War")

Some of the worst [sectarian strife](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sectarian_violence "Sectarian violence") has occurred following the start of the Iraq War,<sup id="cite_ref-Civil_War_20-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Civil_War-20">[20]</a></sup> and continues at least as of 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-Fisher-2016_99-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Fisher-2016-99">[99]</a></sup> The war has featured a cycle of Sunni–Shia revenge killing—Sunni often used car bombs, while Shia favored [death squads](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Death_squad "Death squad").<sup id="cite_ref-146"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-146">[144]</a></sup> As part of its rivalry with Iran, Saudi Arabia spent "tens of billions of dollars" helping Saddam Hussein's war effort.<sup id="cite_ref-147"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-147">[145]</a></sup>

According to one estimate, as of early 2008, 1121 suicide bombers have blown themselves up in Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-148"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-148">[146]</a></sup> Sunni [suicide bombers](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suicide_attack "Suicide attack") have targeted not only thousands of civilians,<sup id="cite_ref-149"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-149">[147]</a></sup> but [mosques](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/2006_al-Askari_Mosque_bombing "2006 al-Askari Mosque bombing"), shrines,<sup id="cite_ref-150"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-150">[148]</a></sup> wedding and funeral processions,<sup id="cite_ref-151"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-151">[149]</a></sup> markets, hospitals, offices, and streets.<sup id="cite_ref-152"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-152">[150]</a></sup> Sunni insurgent organizations include [Ansar al-Islam](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ansar_al-Islam_in_Kurdistan "Ansar al-Islam in Kurdistan").<sup id="cite_ref-153"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-153">[151]</a></sup> Radical groups include [Al-Tawhid Wal-Jihad](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Qaeda_in_Iraq "Al-Qaeda in Iraq"), [Jeish Muhammad](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jeish_Muhammad "Jeish Muhammad"), and [Black Banner Organization](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_Banner_Organization "Black Banner Organization").<sup id="cite_ref-154"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-154">[152]</a></sup>

_[Takfir](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Takfir "Takfir")_ motivation for many of these killings may come from Sunni insurgent leader [Abu Musab al-Zarqawi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abu_Musab_al-Zarqawi "Abu Musab al-Zarqawi"). Before his death Zarqawi was one to quote [Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahhab](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Abd_al-Wahhab "Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahhab"), especially his infamous statement urging followers to kill the Shia of Iraq,<sup id="cite_ref-155"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-155">[153]</a></sup> and calling the Shia "snakes".<sup id="cite_ref-156"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-156">[154]</a></sup>

Another explanation found in his February 2004 open letter to supporters is that by attack Shia he would provoke them to attack Sunnis and thus "awaken" Sunnis who previously had not wanted a sectarian war to join his side. The "cunning" Shia planned to build a state "stretching from Iran through Iraq, Syria and Lebanon" to the Gulf kingdoms, but by attacking Shia in their "religious, political, and military depth" his jihadis would "drag" the Shia "into the arena of sectarian war", and leading them to "bare the teeth of the hidden rancor working in their breasts" and so "awaken the inattentive Sunnis as they feel imminent danger and annihilating death at the hands of theses Sabeans", i.e. Shia.<sup id="cite_ref-Zarqawi_Letter-2004_157-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Zarqawi_Letter-2004-157">[155]</a></sup>

An [al-Qaeda](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Qaeda "Al-Qaeda")\-affiliated website posted a call for "a full-scale war on Shiites all over Iraq, whenever and wherever they are found."<sup id="cite_ref-158"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-158">[156]</a></sup> Suicide bombers continue to attack Iraqi Shia civilians,<sup id="cite_ref-159"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-159">[157]</a></sup> and the Shia ulama have in response declared suicide bombing as _[haraam](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haraam "Haraam")_ (against God, or "forbidden"):

> حتی كسانی كه با انتحار میآيند و میزنند عدهای را میكشند، آن هم به عنوان عملیات انتحاری، اینها در قعر جهنم هستند  
> Even those who kill people with suicide bombing, these shall meet the flames of hell.

Some believe the war has strengthened the [takfir](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Takfir "Takfir") thinking and may spread Sunni–Shia strife elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-161"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-161">[159]</a></sup>

On the Shia side, in early February 2006 militia-dominated government death squads were reportedly "tortur\[ing\] to death or summarily" executing "hundreds" of Sunnis "every month in Baghdad alone," many arrested at random.<sup id="cite_ref-162"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-162">[160]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-163">[161]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-164">[162]</a></sup> According to the British television [Channel 4](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Channel_4 "Channel 4"), from 2005 through early 2006, commandos of the Ministry of the Interior which is controlled by the [Badr Organization](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Badr_Organization "Badr Organization"), and

> ...who are almost exclusively Shia Muslims—have been implicated in rounding up and killing thousands of ordinary Sunni civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-165"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-165">[163]</a></sup>

The violence shows little sign of getting opposite sides to back down. Iran's Shia leaders are said to become "more determined" the more violent the anti-Shia attacks in Iraq become.<sup id="cite_ref-166"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-166">[164]</a></sup> One Shia Grand Ayatollah, Yousef Saanei, who has been described as a moderate, reacted to the 2005 suicide bombings of Shia targets in Iraq by saying the bombers were "wolves without pity" and that "sooner rather than later, Iran will have to put them down".<sup id="cite_ref-baer_167-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-baer-167">[165]</a></sup><sup>[<i><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (May 2023)">better&nbsp;source&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup>

In addition to Iran, Iraq has emerged as a major Shia government when the Twelvers achieved political dominance in 2005 under American occupation. The two communities have often remained separate, mingling regularly only during the Hajj pilgrimage in Mecca. In some countries like Iraq, Syria, Kuwait and Bahrain, communities have mingled and intermarried. Some Shia have complained of mistreatment in countries dominated by Sunnis, especially in Saudi Arabia,<sup id="cite_ref-168"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-168">[166]</a></sup> while some Sunnis have complained of discrimination in the Twelver-dominated states of Iraq and Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-169"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-169">[167]</a></sup>

### Iran [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations&action=edit&section=41 "Edit section: Iran")

Iran is unique in the Muslim world because its population is overwhelmingly more Shia than Sunni (Shia constitute 95% of the population) and because its constitution is [theocratic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theocracy "Theocracy") republic based on rule by a Shia jurist.

The founder of the [Islamic Republic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_republic "Islamic republic"), Ayatollah Ruhollah Khomeini, supported good Sunni–Shia relations. However tension developed between Sunnis and Shia as a result of clashes over Iranian pilgrims and Saudi police at the [hajj](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hajj "Hajj").<sup id="cite_ref-IRIvKSA_170-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-IRIvKSA-170">[168]</a></sup> Millions of Saudi adhere to the school of [Salafism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salafism "Salafism") which is a branch of [Sunni Islam](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sunni_Islam "Sunni Islam").<sup id="cite_ref-171"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-171">[169]</a></sup>

Inside Iran there have been complaints by Sunni of discrimination, particularly in important government positions.<sup id="cite_ref-iran.org_172-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-iran.org-172">[170]</a></sup> In a joint appearance with former Iranian president Akbar Hashemi Rafsanjani calling for Shia-Sunni unity, Sunni Shiekh Yusuf al-Qaradawi complained that no ministers in Iran have been Sunni for a long time, that Sunni officials are scarce even in the regions with majority of Sunni population (such as [Kurdistan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iranian_Kurdistan "Iranian Kurdistan"), or [Balochistan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sistan_and_Baluchestan_Province "Sistan and Baluchestan Province"))<sup id="cite_ref-173"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-173">[171]</a></sup> and despite the presence of [Christian](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christianity "Christianity") churches, as a prominent example of this discrimination. Although reformist President [Mohammad Khatami](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mohammad_Khatami "Mohammad Khatami") promised during his election campaign to build a Sunni mosque in Tehran, none was built during his eight years in office. The president explained the situation by saying Supreme Leader Ayatollah [Ali Khamenei](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ali_Khamenei "Ali Khamenei") would not agree to the proposal.<sup id="cite_ref-174"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-174">[172]</a></sup> As in other parts of the Muslim world, other issues may play a part in the conflict, since most Sunnis in Iran are also ethnic minorities.<sup id="cite_ref-state.gov_175-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-state.gov-175">[173]</a></sup>

Soon after the 1979 revolution, Sunni leaders from Kurdistan, Balouchistan, and [Khorassan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khorasan_Province "Khorasan Province"), set up a new party known as _Shams_, which is short for Shora-ye Markaz-e al Sunaat, to unite Sunnis and lobby for their rights. But six months after that they were closed down, bank accounts suspended and had their leaders arrested by the government on charges that they were backed by Saudi Arabia and Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-iran.org_172-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-iran.org-172">[170]</a></sup>

A [UN](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations "United Nations") [human rights](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_rights "Human rights") report states that:

> ...information indicates Sunnis, along with other religious minorities, are denied by law or practice access to such government positions as cabinet minister, ambassador, provincial governor, mayor and the like, Sunni schools and mosques have been destroyed, and Sunni leaders have been imprisoned, executed and assassinated. The report notes that while some of the information received may be difficult to corroborate there is a clear impression that the right of freedom of religion is not being respected with regard to the Sunni minority.<sup id="cite_ref-176"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-176">[174]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-177">[175]</a></sup>

Members of the 'Balochistan Peoples Front' claim that Sunnis are systematically discriminated against educationally by denial of places at universities, politically by not allowing Sunnis to be army generals, ambassadors, ministers, prime minister, or president, religiously insulting Sunnis in the media, economic discrimination by not giving import or export licenses for Sunni businesses while the majority of Sunnis are left unemployed.<sup id="cite_ref-178"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-178">[176]</a></sup>

There has been a low level resistance in mainly Sunni Iranian Balouchistan against the regime for several years. Official media refers to the fighting as armed clashes between the police and "bandits," "drug-smugglers," and "thugs," to disguise what many believe is essentially a political-religious conflict. [Revolutionary Guards](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_Revolutionary_Guard_Corps "Islamic Revolutionary Guard Corps") have stationed several brigades in Balouchi cities, and have allegedly tracked down and assassinated Sunni leaders both inside Iran and in neighboring Pakistan. In 1996 a leading Sunni, Abdulmalek Mollahzadeh, was gunned down by hitmen, allegedly hired by Tehran, as he was leaving his house in Karachi.<sup id="cite_ref-179"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-179">[177]</a></sup>

Members of Sunni groups in Iran however have been active in what the authorities describe as [terrorist](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terrorism "Terrorism") activities. [Balochi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baloch_people "Baloch people") Sunni [Abdolmalek Rigi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abdolmalek_Rigi "Abdolmalek Rigi") continue to declare the Shia as _[Kafir](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kafir "Kafir")_ and _[Mushrik](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shirk_(Islam) "Shirk (Islam)")_.<sup id="cite_ref-180"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-180">[178]</a></sup> These Sunni groups have been involved in violent activities in Iran and have waged terrorist<sup id="cite_ref-181"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-181">[179]</a></sup> attacks against civilian centers, including an attack next to a girls' school<sup id="cite_ref-182"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-182">[180]</a></sup> according to government sources. The "shadowy Sunni militant group [Jundallah](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jundallah_(Iran) "Jundallah (Iran)")" has reportedly been receiving weaponry from the United States for these attacks according to the semi-official [Fars News Agency](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fars_News_Agency "Fars News Agency").<sup id="cite_ref-183"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-183">[181]</a></sup> The United Nations<sup id="cite_ref-184"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-184">[182]</a></sup> and several countries worldwide have condemned the bombings. _(See [2007 Zahedan bombings](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/2007_Zahedan_bombings "2007 Zahedan bombings") for more information)_

Following the 2005 elections, much of the leadership of Iran has been described as more "staunchly committed to core Shia values" and lacking Ayatollah Khomeini's commitment to Shia–Sunni unity.<sup id="cite_ref-185"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-185">[183]</a></sup> Polemics critical of Sunnis were reportedly being produced in Arabic for dissemination in the Arab Muslim world by [Hojjatieh](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hojjatieh "Hojjatieh")\-aligned elements in the Iranian regime.<sup id="cite_ref-186"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-186">[184]</a></sup> Sunni mosques are not allowed in the capital city of Tehran, and a number of Sunni mosques in other cities have been demolished,<sup id="cite_ref-Iran_Focus_187-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Iran_Focus-187">[185]</a></sup> Sunni literature and teachings are banned in public schools and construction of new Sunni mosques and schools are banned.<sup id="cite_ref-Atlantic_188-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Atlantic-188">[186]</a></sup>

### Syria [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations&action=edit&section=42 "Edit section: Syria")

Syria is approximately three quarters Sunni,<sup id="cite_ref-189"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-189">[187]</a></sup> but its government is predominantly [Alawite](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alawite "Alawite"), a Shia sect that makes up less than 13% of the population. Under [Hafez al-Assad](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hafez_al-Assad "Hafez al-Assad"), Alawites dominated the [Arab Socialist Ba'ath Party](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Socialist_Ba%27ath_Party_%E2%80%93_Syria_Region "Arab Socialist Ba'ath Party – Syria Region"), a secular Arab nationalist party which had ruled Syria under a state of emergency from 1963 to 2011. Alawites are often considered a form of Shia Islam, that differs somewhat from the larger Twelver Shia sect.<sup id="cite_ref-190"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-190">[188]</a></sup>

During the 20th century, an [Islamic uprising in Syria](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_uprising_in_Syria "Islamic uprising in Syria") occurred with sectarian religious overtones between the Alawite-dominated Assad government and the [Islamist](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamism "Islamism") Sunni [Muslim Brotherhood](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muslim_Brotherhood "Muslim Brotherhood"), culminating with the 1982 [Hama massacre](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/1982_Hama_massacre "1982 Hama massacre"). An estimated 10,000 to 40,000 Syrians, mostly civilians, were killed by [Syrian military](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syrian_Armed_Forces "Syrian Armed Forces") in the city. During the uprising, the Sunni Muslim Brotherhood [attacked military cadets](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aleppo_Artillery_School_massacre "Aleppo Artillery School massacre") at an artillery school in [Aleppo](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aleppo "Aleppo"), performed car bomb attacks in Damascus, as well as bomb attacks against the government and its officials, including Hafez al-Assad himself, and had killed several hundred.

How much of the conflict was sparked by Sunni versus Shia divisions and how much by Islamism versus secular-Arab-nationalism, is in question, but according to scholar Vali Nasr the failure of the Ayatollah Khomeini and the Islamic Republic of Iran to support the Muslim Brotherhood against the Baathists "earned \[Khomeini\] the Brotherhood's lasting contempt." It proved to the satisfaction of the Brotherhood that sectarian loyalty trumped Islamist solidarity for Khomeini and eliminated whatever appeal Khomeini might have had to the MB movement as a pan-Islamic leader.<sup id="cite_ref-191"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-191">[189]</a></sup>

#### Syria Civil War [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations&action=edit&section=43 "Edit section: Syria Civil War")

The [Syrian Civil War](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War "Syrian Civil War"), though it started as a political conflict, developed into a struggle between the Alawite-dominated Army and government on the one hand, and the mainly Sunni rebels and former members of the regular army on the other. The casualty toll of the war's first three years has exceeded that of Iraq's decade-long conflict, and the fight has "amplified sectarian tensions to unprecedented levels".<sup id="cite_ref-CFR-SSD_105-7"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-CFR-SSD-105">[105]</a></sup> Rebel groups with 10,000s of Sunni Syrian fighters such as [Ahrar ash-Sham](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ahrar_ash-Sham "Ahrar ash-Sham"), the Islamic Front, and al-Qaeda's [al-Nusra Front](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Nusra_Front "Al-Nusra Front"), employ anti-Shia rhetoric and foreign Arab and Western Sunni fighters have joined the rebels. On the other side Shia from Hezbollah in Lebanon and from Asaib Ahl al-Haq and Kata'ib Hezbollah militias from Iraq have backed the Syrian government.<sup id="cite_ref-CFR-SSD_105-8"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-CFR-SSD-105">[105]</a></sup> "Even Afghan Shia refugees in Iran", driven from Afghanistan by Sunni extremism, have "reportedly been recruited by Tehran for the war in Syria".<sup id="cite_ref-CFR-SSD_105-9"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-CFR-SSD-105">[105]</a></sup>

According to some reports, as of mid-2013, the [Syrian Civil War](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War "Syrian Civil War") has become "overtly sectarian" with the "sectarian lines fall most sharply" between Alawites and Sunnis.<sup id="cite_ref-192"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-192">[190]</a></sup> With the involvement of Lebanese Shia paramilitary group [Hezbollah](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hezbollah "Hezbollah"), the fighting in Syria has reignited "long-simmering tensions between Sunnis and Shi’ites" spilling over into Lebanon and Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-193"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-193">[191]</a></sup> Bulgaria's ex-Ambassador [Dimitar Mihaylov](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Dimitar_Mihaylov&action=edit&redlink=1 "Dimitar Mihaylov (page does not exist)") further claims that the current post-Arab Spring situation (encompassing ISIS, the Syrian civil war, Yemen, Iraq and others) represents a "qualitatively new" development in the history of Shi'a-Sunni dynamics. Historically, the inner rifts within Islamic ideology were to be hidden from the public sphere, while the new violent outbreaks highlight said rift in an obvious manner and is nourished by the two extremes of their mutual rivalry which will strongly affect both globally and regionally.<sup id="cite_ref-test_194-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-test-194">[192]</a></sup>

### Saudi Arabia [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations&action=edit&section=44 "Edit section: Saudi Arabia")

While Shia make up roughly 10% of Saudi Arabia's population,<sup id="cite_ref-195"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-195">[193]</a></sup> they form a large portion of the residents of the [Eastern Province](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Province,_Saudi_Arabia "Eastern Province, Saudi Arabia")—by some estimates a majority<sup id="cite_ref-196"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-196">[194]</a></sup>—where much of the petroleum industry is based. Between 500,000 and a million Shia live there,<sup id="cite_ref-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.236_197-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.236-197">[195]</a></sup> concentrated especially around the oases of [Qatif](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Qatif "Qatif") and [al-Hasa](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Ahsa_Oasis "Al-Ahsa Oasis"). The Majority of Saudi Shia belong to the sect of the Twelvers.<sup id="cite_ref-lcweb2.loc.gov_198-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-lcweb2.loc.gov-198">[196]</a></sup>

The Saudi conflict of Shia and Sunni extends beyond the borders of the kingdom because of international Saudi "[Petro-Islam](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Petro-Islam "Petro-Islam")" influence. Saudi Arabia backed Iraq in the 1980–1988 war with Iran and sponsored militants in Pakistan and Afghanistan who—though primarily targeting the Soviet Union, which had invaded Afghanistan in 1979—also fought to suppress Shia movements.<sup id="cite_ref-sunni-shia-cfr_199-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-sunni-shia-cfr-199">[197]</a></sup>

Relations between the Shia and the Wahhabis are inherently strained because the Wahhabis consider the rituals of the Shia to be the epitome of _shirk_, or [polytheism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polytheism "Polytheism"). In the late 1920s, the [Ikhwan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ikhwan "Ikhwan") ([Ibn Saud's](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ibn_Saud_of_Saudi_Arabia "Ibn Saud of Saudi Arabia") fighting force of converted Wahhabi [Bedouin](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bedouin "Bedouin") Muslims) were particularly hostile to the Shia and demanded that Abd al Aziz forcibly convert them. In response, Abd al Aziz sent Wahhabi missionaries to the [Eastern Province](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Province,_Saudi_Arabia "Eastern Province, Saudi Arabia"), but he did not carry through with attempts at forced conversion. In recent decades the late leading Saudi cleric, [Abd al-Aziz ibn Abd Allah ibn Baaz](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abd_al-Aziz_ibn_Abd_Allah_ibn_Baaz "Abd al-Aziz ibn Abd Allah ibn Baaz"), issued fatwa denouncing Shia as apostates, and according to Shia scholar Vali Nasr "Abdul-Rahman al-Jibrin, a member of the Higher Council of Ulama, even sanctioned the killing of Shia,<sup id="cite_ref-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.236_197-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.236-197">[195]</a></sup> a call that was reiterated by Wahhabi religious literature as late as 2002."<sup id="cite_ref-200"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-200">[198]</a></sup>

Government policy has been to allow Shia their own mosques and to exempt Shia from [Hanbali](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hanbali "Hanbali") inheritance practices.<sup>[<i><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Nevertheless, Shia have been forbidden all but the most modest displays on their principal festivals, which are often occasions of sectarian strife in the Persian Gulf region, with its mixed Sunni–Shia populations.<sup id="cite_ref-lcweb2.loc.gov_198-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-lcweb2.loc.gov-198">[196]</a></sup>

According to a report by the [Human Rights Watch](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_Rights_Watch "Human Rights Watch"):

> Shia Muslims, who constitute about eight percent of the Saudi population, faced discrimination in employment as well as limitations on religious practices. Shia jurisprudence books were banned, the traditional annual Shia mourning procession of Ashura was discouraged, and operating independent Islamic religious establishments remained illegal. At least seven Shi'a religious leaders-Abd al-Latif Muhammad Ali, Habib al-Hamid, Abd al-Latif al-Samin, Abdallah Ramadan, Sa'id al-Bahaar, Muhammad Abd al-Khidair, and Habib Hamdah Sayid Hashim al-Sadah-reportedly remained in prison for violating these restrictions."<sup id="cite_ref-201"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-201">[199]</a></sup>

And [Amnesty International](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amnesty_International "Amnesty International") adds:

> Members of the Shi‘a Muslim community (estimated at between 7 and 10 per cent of Saudi Arabia’s population of about 19 million) suffer systematic political, social, cultural as well as religious discrimination.<sup id="cite_ref-202"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-202">[200]</a></sup>

As of 2006 four of the 150 members of Saudi Arabia's "handpicked" parliament were Shia, but no city had a Shia mayor or police chief, and none of the 300 girls schools for Shia in the Eastern Province had a Shia principal. According to scholar Vali Nasr, Saudi textbooks "characterize Shiism as a form of heresy ... worse than [Christianity](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christianity "Christianity") and [Judaism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Judaism "Judaism")."<sup id="cite_ref-203"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-203">[201]</a></sup>

Forced into exile in the 1970s, Saudi Shia leader [Hassan al-Saffar](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hassan_al-Saffar "Hassan al-Saffar") is said to have been "powerfully influenced" by the works of Sunni Islamists of the Muslim Brotherhood and [Jamaat-e-Islami](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jamaat-e-Islami_Pakistan "Jamaat-e-Islami Pakistan") and by their call for Islamic revolution and an Islamic state.<sup id="cite_ref-204"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-204">[202]</a></sup>

Following the 1979 Iranian Revolution, Shia in Hasa ignored the ban on mourning ceremonies commemorating Ashura. When police broke them up three days of rampage ensued—burned cars, attacked banks, looted shops—centered around Qatif. At least 17 Shia were killed. In February 1980 disturbances were "less spontaneous" and even bloodier.<sup id="cite_ref-205"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-205">[203]</a></sup> Meanwhile, broadcasts from Iran in the name of the Islamic Revolutionary Organization attacked the monarchy, telling listeners, "Kings despoil a country when they enter it and make the noblest of its people its meanest ... This is the nature of monarchy, which is rejected by Islam."<sup id="cite_ref-206"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-206">[204]</a></sup>

By 1993, Saudi Shia had abandoned uncompromising demands and some of al-Saffar's followers met with [King Fahd](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fahd_of_Saudi_Arabia "Fahd of Saudi Arabia") with promises made for reform. In 2005 the new [King Abdullah](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abdullah_of_Saudi_Arabia "Abdullah of Saudi Arabia") also relaxed some restrictions on the Shia.<sup id="cite_ref-207"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-207">[205]</a></sup> However, Shia continue to be arrested for commemorating Ashura as of 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-208"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-208">[206]</a></sup> In December 2006, amidst escalating tensions in Iraq, 38 high ranking Saudi clerics called on Sunni Muslims around the world to "mobilise against Shiites".<sup id="cite_ref-209"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-209">[207]</a></sup> A year later, Shia Grand Ayatollah [Naser Makarem Shirazi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naser_Makarem_Shirazi "Naser Makarem Shirazi") is reported to have responded:

> The Wahhabis ignore the occupation of Islam's first [Qiblah](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Qibla "Qibla") by Israel, and instead focus on declaring [Takfiring](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Takfir "Takfir") fatwas against Shia.<sup id="cite_ref-210"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-210">[208]</a></sup>

-   Saudi Sunni

Another reflection of grassroots Wahhabi or Saudi antipathy to Shia was a statement by Saudi cleric Nasir al-Umar, who accused Iraqi Shia of close ties to the United States and argued that both were enemies of Muslims everywhere.<sup id="cite_ref-211"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-211">[209]</a></sup>

#### Al-Qaeda [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations&action=edit&section=45 "Edit section: Al-Qaeda")

Some Wahabi groups, often labeled as _[takfiri](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Takfiri "Takfiri")_ and sometimes linked to Al-Qaeda, have even advocated the persecution of the Shia as heretics.<sup id="cite_ref-212"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-212">[210]</a></sup> Such groups have been allegedly responsible for violent attacks and suicide bombings at Shi'a gatherings at mosques and shrines, most notably in Iraq during the Ashura mourning ceremonies where hundreds of Shia were killed in coordinated suicide bombings,<sup id="cite_ref-213"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-213">[211]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-214">[212]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-215"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-215">[213]</a></sup> but also in Pakistan and Afghanistan. However, in a video message, Al-Qaeda deputy Dr [Ayman al-Zawahiri](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ayman_al-Zawahiri "Ayman al-Zawahiri") directed Abu Musab al-Zarqawi, of Al-Qaeda in Iraq, not to attack civilian targets but to focus on the occupation troops. His call seems to have been ignored, or swept away in the increasing tensions of Iraq under occupation.

#### Hajj [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations&action=edit&section=46 "Edit section: Hajj")

Every year, Muslims from all over the world attend the hajj pilgrimage in Mecca in Western Saudi Arabia. Shia had complained off and on of mistreatment by the Sunnis who ran Mecca and the hajj ceremonies. Following the advent of Saudi-Wahhabi rule over Mecca in 1924 tensions between Shia and Sunni increased. To the fury of Shia Muslims, the Wahhabi Sunnis demolished domes in the [cemetery of Al-Baqi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Baqi_Cemetery "Al-Baqi Cemetery"), near the Medina, "the reputed resting place of the Prophet Muhammad’s daughter Fatima and four of the Twelve Imams".<sup id="cite_ref-Kramer-ME_57-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Kramer-ME-57">[57]</a></sup> In 1943, a Saudi religious judge ordered the beheading of an Iranian pilgrim "for allegedly defiling the Great Mosque with excrement" smuggled into "the mosque in his pilgrim’s garment". Saudi public opinion considered the crime unsurprising and the punishment just, Iranian were furious and demanded payment of an indemnity. Tensions lowered again during the 1960s, when pious/tradionalist Muslims set aside differences in the face of the rising popularity of Nasser's leftist Arab nationalism. Pilgrims from Iran (mostly Shia) rose in number from 12,000 in 1961 to 57,000 in 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-Kramer-ME_57-5"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Kramer-ME-57">[57]</a></sup>

In 1987, about seven years after the [Iranian Revolution](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iranian_Revolution "Iranian Revolution"), Mecca became a site "[of unprecedented carnage](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/1987_Mecca_incident "1987 Mecca incident")" when demonstrating Shia Iranian pilgrims clashed with Saudi security forces and over four hundred were killed. The Saudis and their supporters claimed violent Iranian demonstrators crushed themselves to death in a stampede of their own making. The Iranians and their sympathizers claimed the Saudis had conspired to provoke and then shoot Iranian pilgrims. The pilgrimage to Mecca, where violence is forbidden, had itself become a point of confrontation between rival visions of Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-Kramer-ME_57-6"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Kramer-ME-57">[57]</a></sup>

### Lebanon [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations&action=edit&section=47 "Edit section: Lebanon")

Though sectarian tensions in Lebanon were at their height during the [Lebanese Civil War](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lebanese_Civil_War "Lebanese Civil War"), the Shia–Sunni relations were not the main conflict of the war. The Shia party/militia of [Hezbollah](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hezbollah "Hezbollah") emerged in Lebanon during the Lebanese Civil War as one of the strongest forces following the Israeli withdrawal in the year 2000, and the collapse of the [South Lebanese Army](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Lebanese_Army "South Lebanese Army") in the South. The tensions blew into a [limited warfare](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/2008_conflict_in_Lebanon "2008 conflict in Lebanon") between Shia dominated and Sunni dominated political alliances in 2008.

With the eruption of the Syrian Civil War, [tensions](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bab_al-Tibbaneh,_Jabal_Mohsen_clashes "Bab al-Tibbaneh, Jabal Mohsen clashes") increased between the Shia-affiliated Alawites and Sunnis of Tripoli, erupting twice into deadly violence—in June 2011, and the [second time in February 2012](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/2012_sectarian_clashes_in_Lebanon "2012 sectarian clashes in Lebanon"). The Syrian war has affected Hezbollah, which was once lauded by both Sunnis and Shi'ites for its battles against Israel, but now has lost support from many Sunnis for its military assistance to Syrian President Bashar al-Assad.

<sup id="cite_ref-yahoo_216-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-yahoo-216">[214]</a></sup> The bombings are thought to be in retaliation<sup id="cite_ref-dozens_217-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-dozens-217">[215]</a></sup> for a large car bomb which detonated on 15 August 2013 and killed at least 24 and wounded hundreds in a part of Beirut controlled by the Hezbollah<sup id="cite_ref-bassam_218-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-bassam-218">[216]</a></sup>

### Jordan [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations&action=edit&section=48 "Edit section: Jordan")

Although the country of Jordan is 95% Sunni and has not seen any Shia–Sunni fighting within, it has played a part in the recent Shia-Sunni strife. It is the home country of anti-Shia insurgent [Raed Mansour al-Banna](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Raed_Mansour_al-Banna&action=edit&redlink=1 "Raed Mansour al-Banna (page does not exist)"), who died perpetrating one of Iraq's worst suicide bombings in the city of Al-Hillah. Al-Banna killed 125 Shia and wounded another 150 in the [2005 Al Hillah bombing](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/2005_Al_Hillah_bombing "2005 Al Hillah bombing") of a police recruiting station and adjacent open air market. In March 2005 [Salt](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salt,_Jordan "Salt, Jordan"), al-Banna's home town, saw a three-day wake for al-Banna who Jordanian newspapers and celebrants proclaimed a [martyr](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shahid "Shahid") to Islam, which by definition made the Shia victims "infidels whose murder was justified." Following the wake Shia mobs in Iraq attacked the Jordanian embassy on 20 March 2005. Ambassadors were withdrawn from both countries.<sup id="cite_ref-Post2005_219-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Post2005-219">[217]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.227-8_220-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.227-8-220">[218]</a></sup> All this resulted despite the strong filial bonds, ties of commerce, and traditional friendship between the two neighboring countries.<sup id="cite_ref-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.227-8_220-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Nasr,_Vali_2006_p.227-8-220">[218]</a></sup>

### Egypt [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations&action=edit&section=49 "Edit section: Egypt")

According to Pew, roughly 99% of Egyptian Muslims regarded themselves as [Sunni Muslims](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sunni_Muslims "Sunni Muslims").<sup id="cite_ref-221"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-221">[219]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pbs_222-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-pbs-222">[220]</a></sup> others put the number of Shia somewhere between 800,000<sup id="cite_ref-223"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-223">[221]</a></sup> to about two to three million.<sup id="cite_ref-ipsnews_224-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-ipsnews-224">[222]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sky-news_225-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-sky-news-225">[223]</a></sup> The Syrian Civil War has brought on an increase in anti-Shia rhetoric,<sup id="cite_ref-mob_226-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-mob-226">[224]</a></sup> and what Human Rights Watch states is "anti-Shia hate speech by Salafis".<sup id="cite_ref-HRW_227-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-HRW-227">[225]</a></sup> In 2013 a mob of several hundred attacked a house in the village of Abu Musallim near Cairo, dragging four Shia worshipers through the street before lynching them.<sup id="cite_ref-HRW_227-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-HRW-227">[225]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mob_226-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-mob-226">[224]</a></sup>

### Yemen [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations&action=edit&section=50 "Edit section: Yemen")

Muslims in Yemen include the majority Shafi'i (Sunni) and the minority Zaidi (Shia). Zaidi are sometimes called "Fiver Shia" instead of Twelver Shia because they recognize the first four of the Twelve Imams but accept [Zayd ibn Ali](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zayd_ibn_Ali "Zayd ibn Ali") as their "Fifth Imām" rather than his brother [Muhammad al-Baqir](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muhammad_al-Baqir "Muhammad al-Baqir"). Shia–Sunni conflict in Yemen involves the [Shia insurgency](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia_insurgency_in_Yemen "Shia insurgency in Yemen") in northern Yemen.<sup id="cite_ref-scribd.com_18-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-scribd.com-18">[18]</a></sup>

Both Shia and Sunni dissidents in Yemen have similar complaints about the government—cooperation with the American government and an alleged failure to following Sharia law<sup id="cite_ref-228"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-228">[226]</a></sup>—but it's the Shia who have allegedly been singled out for government crackdown.

During and after the US-led invasion of Iraq, members of the Zaidi-Shia community protested after Friday prayers every week outside mosques, particularly the [Grand Mosque](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Sana%27a "Great Mosque of Sana'a") in [Sana'a](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sana%27a "Sana'a"), during which they shouted anti-US and anti-Israeli slogans, and criticised the government's close ties to America.<sup id="cite_ref-229"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-229">[227]</a></sup> These protests were led by ex-parliament member and Imam, Bader Eddine al-Houthi.<sup id="cite_ref-230"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-230">[228]</a></sup> In response the Yemeni government has implemented a campaign to crush to the Zaidi-Shia rebellion"<sup id="cite_ref-231"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-231">[229]</a></sup> and harass journalists.<sup id="cite_ref-232"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-232">[230]</a></sup>

These latest measures come as the government faces a Sunni rebellion with a similar motivation to the Zaidi discontent.<sup id="cite_ref-233"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-233">[231]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-234"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-234">[232]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-235"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-235">[233]</a></sup>

A March 2015 suicide bombing of two mosques (used mainly by supporters of the [Zaidi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zaidiyyah "Zaidiyyah") Shia-led [Houthi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Houthi "Houthi") rebel movement), in the Yemeni capital of [Sanaa](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sana%27a "Sana'a"), killed at least 137 people and wounded 300. The Sunni [Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant "Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant") movement claimed responsibility, issuing a statement saying: "Let the polytheist Houthis know that the soldiers of the Islamic State will not rest until we have uprooted them." Both the Sunni [al-Qaeda](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Qaeda "Al-Qaeda") and "Islamic State" consider Shia Muslims to be heretics.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC-yemen_236-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-BBC-yemen-236">[234]</a></sup>

### Bahrain [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations&action=edit&section=51 "Edit section: Bahrain")

The small [Persian Gulf](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Persian_Gulf "Persian Gulf") island state of Bahrain has a Shia majority but is ruled by Sunni [Al Khalifa family](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al_Khalifa_family "Al Khalifa family") as a [constitutional monarchy](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy "Constitutional monarchy"), with Sunni dominating the ruling class and military and disproportionately represented in the business and landownership.<sup id="cite_ref-237"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-237">[235]</a></sup> According to the CIA World Factbook, [Al Wefaq](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al_Wefaq "Al Wefaq") the largest Shia political society, won the largest number of seats in the elected chamber of the legislature. However, Shia discontent has resurfaced in recent years with street demonstrations and occasional low-level violence."<sup id="cite_ref-238"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-238">[236]</a></sup> Bahrain has many disaffected unemployed youths and many have protested Sheikh [Hamad bin Isa Al Khalifa](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hamad_bin_Isa_Al_Khalifa "Hamad bin Isa Al Khalifa")'s efforts to create a parliament as merely a "cooptation of the [effendis](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effendi "Effendi")", i.e. traditional elders and notables. Bahrain's 2002 election was widely boycotted by Shia. Mass demonstrations have been held in favor of full-fledged democracy in March and June 2005, against an alleged insult to Ayatollah Khamenei in July 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-239"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-239">[237]</a></sup>

### Pakistan [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations&action=edit&section=52 "Edit section: Pakistan")

Pakistan's citizens have had serious Shia-Sunni discord. Almost 90% of Pakistan's Muslim population is Sunni, with 10% being Shia, but this Shia minority forms the second largest Shia population of any country,<sup id="cite_ref-240"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-240">[238]</a></sup> larger than the Shia majority in Iraq.

Until recently Shia–Sunni relations have been cordial, and a majority of people of both sects participated in the creation the state of Pakistan in the 1940s.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_17-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-auto-17">[17]</a></sup> Despite the fact that Pakistan is a Sunni majority country, Shia have been elected to top offices and played an important part in the country's politics. Several top Pakistani military and political figures such as General [Muhammad Musa](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muhammad_Musa "Muhammad Musa"), and [Pakistan's President](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/President_of_Pakistan "President of Pakistan") [Yahya Khan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yahya_Khan "Yahya Khan")<sup>[<i><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> were Shia, as well as Former President [Asif Ali Zardari](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asif_Ali_Zardari "Asif Ali Zardari") was a Shia. There are many intermarriages between Shia and Sunnis in Pakistan.

Unfortunately, from 1987 to 2007, "as many as 4,000 people are estimated to have died" in Shia-Sunni sectarian fighting in Pakistan",<sup id="cite_ref-csmonitor.com_241-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-csmonitor.com-241">[239]</a></sup> another estimate is nearly 4,000 people have been killed and 6,800 injured from the beginning of 2000 to 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-FAIR-2014_242-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-FAIR-2014-242">[240]</a></sup>

Amongst the culprits blamed for the killing are Al-Qaeda working "with local sectarian groups" to kill what they perceive as Shia [apostates](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apostasy_in_Islam "Apostasy in Islam"), and "foreign powers ... trying to sow discord."<sup id="cite_ref-csmonitor.com_241-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-csmonitor.com-241">[239]</a></sup> Most violence takes place in the largest province of [Punjab](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Punjab_region "Punjab region") and the country's commercial and financial capital, [Karachi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karachi "Karachi").<sup id="cite_ref-Pakistan's_Shia-Sunni_divide_243-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Pakistan's_Shia-Sunni_divide-243">[241]</a></sup> There have also been conflagrations in the provinces of [Khyber Pakhtunkhwa](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khyber_Pakhtunkhwa "Khyber Pakhtunkhwa"), [Balochistan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Balochistan "Balochistan") and [Azad Kashmir](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Azad_Kashmir "Azad Kashmir"),<sup id="cite_ref-Pakistan's_Shia-Sunni_divide_243-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Pakistan's_Shia-Sunni_divide-243">[241]</a></sup> with several hundreds of Shia killed in Balochistan killed since 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-hrw-baluchistan_244-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-hrw-baluchistan-244">[242]</a></sup> Shia have responded to attacks creating a classic vicious cycle of "outrages and vengeance".<sup id="cite_ref-245"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-245">[243]</a></sup>

Arab states especially Saudi Arabia and [GCC](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cooperation_Council_for_the_Arab_States_of_the_Gulf "Cooperation Council for the Arab States of the Gulf") states have been funding extremist [Deobandi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deobandi "Deobandi") Sunnis and [Wahhabis](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wahhabi "Wahhabi") in Pakistan, since the [Afghan Jihad](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Afghan_War "Soviet–Afghan War").<sup id="cite_ref-246"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-246">[244]</a></sup> Whereas Iran has been funding Shia militant groups such as [Sipah-e-Muhammad Pakistan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sipah-e-Muhammad_Pakistan "Sipah-e-Muhammad Pakistan"), resulting in tit-for-tat attacks on each other.<sup id="cite_ref-Pakistan's_Shia-Sunni_divide_243-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Pakistan's_Shia-Sunni_divide-243">[241]</a></sup> Pakistan has become a battleground between Saudi Arabia-funded Deobandi Sunni and Wahhabis and Iran-funded Shia resulting in the deaths of thousands of innocent Muslims.

#### Background [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations&action=edit&section=53 "Edit section: Background")

Some see a precursor of Pakistani Shia–Sunni strife in the April 1979 execution of deposed President Zulfikar Ali Bhutto on questionable charges by [Islamic fundamentalist](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_fundamentalism "Islamic fundamentalism") General [Muhammad Zia-ul-Haq](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muhammad_Zia-ul-Haq "Muhammad Zia-ul-Haq"). Ali Bhutto was Shia, Zia ul-Haq a Sunni.<sup id="cite_ref-247"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-247">[245]</a></sup>

[Zia-ul-Haq's Islamization](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zia-ul-Haq%27s_Islamization "Zia-ul-Haq's Islamization") that followed was resisted by Shia who saw it as "Sunnification" as the laws and regulations were based on Sunni _[fiqh](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fiqh "Fiqh")_. In July 1980, 25,000 Shia protested the [Islamization](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamization "Islamization") laws in the capital [Islamabad](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamabad "Islamabad"). Further exacerbating the situation was the dislike between Shia leader Imam Khomeini and General Zia ul-Haq.<sup id="cite_ref-248"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-248">[246]</a></sup>

Shia formed student associations and a Shia party, Sunni began to form sectarian militias recruited from Deobandi and [Ahl al-Hadith](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ahl_al-Hadith "Ahl al-Hadith") madrasahs. Preaching against the Shia in Pakistan was cleric [Israr Ahmed](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israr_Ahmed "Israr Ahmed"). [Manzoor Nomani](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manzoor_Nomani "Manzoor Nomani"), a senior Indian cleric with close ties to Saudi Arabia published a book entitled _Iranian Revolution: Imam Khomeini and Shiism_. The book, which "became the gospel of Deobandi militants" in the 1980s, attacked Khomeini and argued the excesses of the Islamic revolution were proof that Shiism was not the doctrine of misguided brothers, but beyond the Islamic pale.<sup id="cite_ref-249"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-249">[247]</a></sup>

Anti-Shia groups in Pakistan include the [Lashkar-e-Jhangvi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lashkar-e-Jhangvi "Lashkar-e-Jhangvi") and [Sipah-e-Sahaba Pakistan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sipah-e-Sahaba_Pakistan "Sipah-e-Sahaba Pakistan"), offshoots of the [Jamiat Ulema-e-Islam](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jamiat_Ulema-e-Islam "Jamiat Ulema-e-Islam") (JUI). The groups demand the expulsion of all Shia from Pakistan and have killed hundreds of Pakistani Shia between 1996 and 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-Rashid,_Taliban_2000,_p.194_250-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Rashid,_Taliban_2000,_p.194-250">[248]</a></sup> As in Iraq they "targeted Shia in their holy places and mosques, especially during times of communal prayer."<sup id="cite_ref-251"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-251">[249]</a></sup> From January to May 1997, Sunni terror groups assassinated 75 Shia community leaders "in a systematic attempt to remove Shia from positions of authority."<sup id="cite_ref-252"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-252">[250]</a></sup> Lashkar-e-Jhangvi has declared Shia to be "American agents" and the "near enemy" in global jihad.<sup id="cite_ref-253"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-253">[251]</a></sup>

An example of an early Shia–Sunni _[fitna](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fitna_(word) "Fitna (word)")_ shootout occurred in [Kurram](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kurram_Valley "Kurram Valley"), one of the [tribal agencies](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federally_Administered_Tribal_Areas "Federally Administered Tribal Areas") of the [Northwest Pakistan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khyber_Pakhtunkhwa "Khyber Pakhtunkhwa"), where the [Pushtun](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pashtun_people "Pashtun people") population was split between Sunnis and Shia. In September 1996 more than 200 people were killed when a gun battle between teenage Shia and Sunni escalated into a communal war that lasted five days. Women and children were kidnapped and gunmen even executed out-of-towners who were staying at a local hotel.<sup id="cite_ref-254"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-254">[252]</a></sup>

"Over 80,000 Pakistani Islamic militants have trained and fought with the Taliban since 1994. They form a hardcore of Islamic activists, ever-ready to carry out a similar Taliban-style Islamic revolution in Pakistan.", according to Pakistani journalist [Ahmed Rashid](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ahmed_Rashid "Ahmed Rashid").<sup id="cite_ref-Rashid,_Taliban_2000,_p.194_250-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-Rashid,_Taliban_2000,_p.194-250">[248]</a></sup>

### Afghanistan [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations&action=edit&section=54 "Edit section: Afghanistan")

Shia–Sunni strife in Pakistan is strongly intertwined with that in Afghanistan. The anti-Shia Afghan [Taliban](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taliban "Taliban") regime helped anti-Shia Pakistani groups and vice versa. Lashkar-e-Jhangvi and Sipah-e-Sahaba Pakistan, have sent thousands of volunteers to fight with the Taliban regime and "in return the Taliban gave sanctuary to their leaders in the Afghan capital of Kabul."<sup id="cite_ref-255"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-255">[253]</a></sup>

Shia–Sunni strife inside of Afghanistan has been between the Sunni Taliban and Shia Afghans, primarily the [Hazara](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hazara_people "Hazara people") ethnic group—a function of the puritanical religious character of the Taliban and their "traditional [Pashtun](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pashtuns "Pashtuns") biases against Shias".<sup id="cite_ref-256"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-256">[254]</a></sup>

In 1998 more than 8,000 noncombatants were killed when the Taliban [attacked](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battles_of_Mazar-i-Sharif_1997-1998#Recapture_of_Mazar-e_Sharif "Battles of Mazar-i-Sharif 1997-1998") [Mazar-i-Sharif](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mazar-i-Sharif "Mazar-i-Sharif") and [Bamiyan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bamyan,_Afghanistan "Bamyan, Afghanistan") where many Hazaras live.<sup id="cite_ref-257"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations#cite_note-257">[255]</a></sup> Some of the slaughter was indiscriminate, but many were Shia targeted by the Taliban.
